وقد قيل أيضًا في التمييز بين الذاتي والعرضي: «إن الذاتي عبارة عما يكون مقوما للشيء، والعرضي عما لا يكون مقوما»، وفيه من الكلام فإنه أي المقوم- يتناول من الذاتيات ما لا يكون / وإلا على الماهية؛ إذ المقوم مقوم لغيره لا محالة؛ اللهم إلا أن يكون المراد بالمقوم ما لا يفهم من ظاهر لفظه بل هو الذي مرَّ ذكره من قبل.
وقيل أيضًا: «إن الذاتي هو الذي لا يفارقه في الوجود، والعرضي هو الذي يفارقه، وإنه لا يكون كما ينبغي كذلك. فإن كيثرًا من الأشياء كذلك ولا يكون ذاتياً مثل السواد الزنجي ونحو ذلك بل الذاتي هو الذي سبق ذكره، وهو أنه إذا فهم معناه ومعنى الذي هو ذاتي له لا يمكن أن يفهم ذات ذلك الشيء إلا وأن يفهم ذاته أو لا كالحيوان بالنسبة إلى الإنسان، فإن الإنسان من حيث هو الإنسان لا يمكن تعقله بدون الحيوان.
ثم الذي لا يعلم مع ما هو من جزئياته إلا وأن يعلم معه ثلاثة أشياء: أحدها أن الجزئي له ذلك المعنى بالضرورة، وثانيها أن الذاتي مقوم على ما هو ذاتي له من الجزئيات، وثالثها أنه لا يكون مستفادًا من الغير. فإن الإنسان لا يكون حيوانا بالسبب الخارجي الذي جعله حيوانا، بل لذاته هو حيوان؛ إذ لو كان لعلة لأمكن فرضه إنسانًا غير حيوان عند فرض عدم العلة؛ وليس هذا مصيرًا إلى إن الحيوان وجد بذاته من غير علة أو جدته بل المراد أن شيئًا ما لم يجعل الإنسان حيوانا.
ثم الذي جعل الإنسان فقد جعل الحيوان لجعله الإنسان؛ لأن الإنسان حيوان ما لكن لا يمكن أن يجعل الإنسان. ثم إفادة الحيوانية وإلا لكانت الإنسانية مقومة دون الحيوانية، ثم الحيوانية تكون واردة عليه من خارج وذلك محال؛ وهذا من جملة ما يشاركه فيه اللوازم التي يلزم الشيء لماهيته لا في وجوده كالفردية للثلاثة مثلا. فإن الفردية لها لا يكون بعلة بل هي في نفسها وماهيتها لا تكون إلا فردًا، ففرق بين أن يوجد الشيء شيئًا وبين أن يوجد لشيء؛ إذ المفهوم من قولنا يوجده لشيء أن يوجد ذلك الشيء أولاً. ثم يفيد ذلك الشيء الآخر، والمفهوم من ذلك القول لا يكون كذلك، وهذا ظاهر.
That necessary [attribute], even if it cannot occur in concrete individuals and the necessary has been removed from it. So it has become clear to you from this that among attributes are those whose removal is valid in existence, and among them are those whose removal is valid in imagination, and among them are those whose removal is not valid at all and it is accidental, and among them are those whose removal is not valid and it is essential. But it is distinguished from the accidental by the fact that the mind necessitates the priority of the essential to the thing over the thing, unlike the accidental as is known. And it has also been said in the distinction between the essential and the accidental: 'The essential is an expression for what is constitutive of the thing, and the accidental is what is not constitutive,' and there is discussion in it, because 'constitutive' includes from the essentials what is not [constitutive] of the quiddity? Since the constitutive constitutes something else necessarily; unless what is meant by 'constitutive' is not understood from its apparent wording but is what was mentioned previously. And it was also said: 'The essential is what does not separate from it in existence, and the accidental is what separates from it,' and it is not as it should be. For many things are like that but are not essential, such as the blackness of a Zanjī and the like. Rather the essential is what was mentioned previously, which is: when one understands its meaning and the meaning of that which is essential to it, one cannot understand the essence of that thing except by understanding its essence or not, like 'animal' in relation to 'human', for 'human' insofar as it is human cannot be conceived without 'animal'. Then, that which is not known with what is among its particulars except that three things are known with it: first, that the particular necessarily has that meaning; second, that the essential is constitutive of what is essential to it of the particulars; third, that it is not acquired from another. For 'human' is not 'animal' by an external cause that made it animal, but by its essence it is animal. For if it were by a cause, it would be possible to suppose it human without animal by supposing the absence of the cause. And this is not to say that 'animal' exists by itself without a cause, but rather the meaning is that nothing made 'human' an animal. Then, what made 'human' has made 'animal' by making 'human', because 'human' is animal- what, but it cannot make 'human'. Then, the benefit of animality, otherwise 'humanity' would be constitutive without animality, then animality would be something coming to it from outside, and that is impossible. And this is among the things that share with it the necessary concomitants that accompany a thing due to its quiddity, not in its existence, like 'oddness' for 'three' for example. For oddness for it is not by a cause, but it is in itself and its quiddity only as odd. So there is a difference between something being something and something being for something; for the meaning of our saying 'it makes it something' is that that thing exists first, then it benefits that other thing, whereas the meaning of that saying is not like that, and this is apparent.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage is a detailed definitional and argumentative discussion on the distinction between essential (dhātī) and accidental ('araḍī) attributes. The author begins by summarizing the previous discussion: among attributes, some can be removed in existence, some can be removed in imagination, some cannot be removed at all (accidental), and some cannot be removed (essential). The essential is distinguished from the accidental by the mind's recognition that the essential has priority over the thing. The author then presents and critiques two common criteria for distinguishing essential from accidental. The first criterion states that the essential is constitutive (muqawwim) of the thing, while the accidental is not. The author notes that this definition is problematic because 'constitutive' usually means constituting something else, not the thing itself. Unless 'constitutive' is understood in a specialized sense previously mentioned. The second criterion states that the essential does not separate from the thing in existence, while the accidental does. The author rejects this as insufficient, giving the example of the blackness of a Zanjī person (which is inseparable but accidental). The correct definition is then given: when one understands the meaning of the essential and what it is essential to, one cannot understand the essence of that thing without also understanding the essential. For example, 'human' cannot be conceived without 'animal'. Then the author adds that for a particular to have an essential attribute, three things must hold: (1) the particular necessarily has that meaning, (2) the essential is constitutive of that particular, and (3) the essential is not acquired from an external cause. A proof is given for (3): if 'human' were 'animal' by an external cause, then it would be possible to suppose a human without animal when the cause is removed. But this is impossible. Furthermore, if something made 'human' an animal, then by making 'human' it would also make 'animal', which is absurd because 'human' already includes animality. The author distinguishes this from necessary concomitants (lawāzim) that follow from a thing's quiddity, like oddness for the number three, which are not caused externally. The passage concludes with a clarification of the difference between 'something existing as something' and 'something existing for something'.

تعقل
مقوم

A) Blackness (like Zanjī)
B) Animality
C) Laughing
D) Standing

Key Takeaway
Essential attributes are those without which the thing cannot be conceived, not those that never leave it in existence.

Essential vs Necessary Concomitant (Lāzim)
Author distinguishes essential attributes from necessary concomitants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish essential attributes from necessary concomitants (lawāzim) and explain the difference between 'something existing as something' and 'something existing for something'.

Core Idea
Necessary concomitants follow from the quiddity but are not essential; they are not caused externally either.

The author concludes by distinguishing essential attributes from necessary concomitants (lawāzim) that accompany a thing due to its quiddity, like oddness for the number three. Both are not caused externally, but there is a difference: the essential is constitutive, while the concomitant is not. This lesson explores that subtle distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the distinction introduced
The author says: 'وهذا من جملة ما يشاركه فيه اللوازم التي يلزم الشيء لماهيته لا في وجوده كالفردية للثلاثة' (this is among the things that share with it the necessary concomitants that accompany a thing due to its quiddity, not in its existence, like oddness for three).
Necessary concomitants are also not caused externally, but they are not essential.
2
Understand the similarity and difference
Both essential attributes and necessary concomitants are not acquired from an external cause. However, essential attributes are constitutive of the thing's quiddity (e.g., animal for human), while necessary concomitants follow from the quiddity but are not part of it (e.g., oddness is a property of three but three is not odd? Actually three is odd, but oddness is a property that necessarily accompanies three; still, oddness is not part of the definition of three).
The author says 'فالفردية لها لا يكون بعلة' (oddness for it is not by a cause).
3
Analyze the 'existing as' vs 'existing for' distinction
The author introduces a subtle distinction: 'ففرق بين أن يوجد الشيء شيئًا وبين أن يوجد لشيء' (there is a difference between something being something and something being for something). For essential attributes, the thing exists as that attribute (e.g., human is animal). For necessary concomitants, the attribute exists for the thing (e.g., oddness is for three).
This distinction is crucial: essential attributes are identical (in a sense) to the thing's quiddity, while concomitants are additional properties that follow necessarily.
4
Apply to Islamic examples
Consider 'the world is contingent' (ḥudūth). Is 'contingency' an essential attribute of the world or a necessary concomitant? In Kalam, the world is defined as 'temporarily originated' (ḥādith), so contingency may be essential. But 'being created' might be a necessary concomitant. Think about definitions.
This distinction helps in constructing accurate definitions.

Source Text

فنا الكبير
ذلك اللازم، وإن كان لا يمكن أن يحصل في الأعيان وقد سلب عنه اللازم. فقد بان لك إذن من هذا أن من الصفات ما يصح سلبه وجودًا، ومنها ما يصح سلبه توهما، ومنها ما لا يصح سلبه بوجه وهو عارض، ومنها ما لا يصح سلبه وهو ذاتي. لكن يتميز من العارض بأن الذهن ما يوجب سبق الذاتي للشيء على الشيء بخلاف العارض على ما عرف.
وقد قيل أيضًا في التمييز بين الذاتي والعرضي: «إن الذاتي عبارة عما يكون مقوما للشيء، والعرضي عما لا يكون مقوما»، وفيه من الكلام فإنه أي المقوم- يتناول من الذاتيات ما لا يكون / وإلا على الماهية؛ إذ المقوم مقوم لغيره لا محالة؛ اللهم إلا أن يكون المراد بالمقوم ما لا يفهم من ظاهر لفظه بل هو الذي مرَّ ذكره من قبل.

Why an Individual Cannot Change Species
Author argues against species change for individuals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why, according to this logic text, a specific individual (like Zayd) cannot change into another species (like a horse).

Core Idea
An individual's matter and form are inseparable; species change is impossible for existing individuals.

In logic and metaphysics, it is crucial to understand that individuals are not bundles of accidental properties. This passage from a logic text argues that the very identity of an individual animal—say, Zayd—is constituted by a specific matter and form. Changing species would require replacing both, which would destroy the individual. Let's unpack the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being defended
The author claims that an individual animal cannot change its species; e.g., Zayd cannot become a horse. Look for the statement that this is impossible.
The claim is explicitly against the possibility of species change.
2
Extract the conditional structure
The author presents a conditional: either the individual is completed by its matter and form (and thus is that individual), or it is not and ceases to be. Why does he reject the alternative that species could change via accidental properties?
The rejection hinges on the inseparability of matter and form.
3
Analyze the role of matter and form
The author says that what makes Zayd a man also makes him the particular animal he is. How does this tie to the impossibility of species change?
The same act that gives animality gives humanity; they are not separate.
4
Extend the reasoning to other species
The same logic applies to horses: horseness cannot become humanity. What does this imply about the relationship between form and individual identity?
Each species has its own specific form that defines its individuals.
5
Understand the logician's task
The author concludes that the logician's job is not to give examples for every species but to provide the general law. Why is this methodological point important?
It ensures the principle is universal and not limited by examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فذلك أن يقال إن ذلك الحيوان الذي هو الإنسان إنما يكون من مادة وصورة بحسبه. وأما أن يتم بكونه منهما فيكون ذلك الإنسان بعينه، أو لا يتم فلا يكون لا ذلك الإنسان ولا ذلك الحيوان وليس يحتمل التقدير الآخر، وهو أنه إنما يصير إنسانًا بلواحق تلحق مادته إذا فرضنا عدمها وعروض أضدادها ليكون حيوانا غير إنسان. فإنه لا يمكن أن يصير إنسانًا بما يعرض في مادته المستعدة للحيوانية حتى إذا لم يكن هو لم يكن إنسانًا؛ بل إنما جعله إنسانًا عين ما جعله إنسانًا، فجعله للحيوانية هو جعله للإنسانية، أعني حيوانية زيد وإنسانيته. وعلى هذا في كل فرد من كل نوع : فإن الحيوان الذي هو الفرس مثلا، إنما يكونه من مادة وصورة بحسبه كما في الإنسان. فالحيوان الذي هو الفرس لا يمكن أن تتبدل فرسيته بالإنسانية، حتى إذا فرضنا زوال الفرسية وعروض الإنسانية فذلك الحيوان هو ذلك الحيوان بعينه ؛ بل الذي هو الفرس فهو بعينه ذلك الفرس. وذلك الحيوان كذلك فلا يمكن أن يكون على خلاف ذلك أصلا، يعني أن يكون إنسانًا يدل كونه فرسًا أو فرسا يدل كونه إنسانًا. وكيف يمكن؟ وإنه لا يمكن أن يوجد إلا وأن يوجد إنسانًا أو فرسًا أو غير ذلك من أنواعه فلو وجد إنسانًا مثلا، فلا يمكن أن يوجد فرسًا وهو إنسان، وكذلك إذا وجد فرسًا أو غير ذلك. وأما التعرض للمثال في كل نوع من كل جنس فليس ذلك على المنطقي؛ بل عليه إعطاء القانون الذي يعتدي به في الأمثلة وأجزاء حكمه فيها إن كانت على وفق موجبها.
Thus it is said that the animal which is man comes to be from matter and form specific to him. Either he is completed by being from them so that he is that very man, or he is not completed so that he is neither that man nor that animal. And the other consideration is not possible, namely that he becomes a man by accidents that attach to his matter if we suppose their absence and the occurrence of their opposites so that he becomes an animal other than man. For it is not possible that he becomes a man by what occurs in his matter prepared for animality such that if he were not he would not be a man; rather, what made him a man is precisely what made him a man, so making him for animality is making him for humanity—I mean Zayd's animality and his humanity. And likewise for every individual of every species: the animal that is, for example, the horse comes to be from matter and form specific to it as in man. The animal that is the horse cannot have its horseness changed into humanity, such that if we suppose the removal of horseness and the occurrence of humanity, that animal is that very animal; rather, that which is the horse is precisely that horse. And that animal is likewise such that it cannot be otherwise at all—meaning that it is a man indicating that it is a horse, or a horse indicating that it is a man. How could it be? And it cannot exist except as a man or a horse or other of its species; so if it exists as a man, for example, it cannot exist as a horse while being a man, and likewise if it exists as a horse or other. As for working out examples for every species of every genus, that is not incumbent on the logician; rather, he must give the law by which to proceed in the examples and the parts of its judgment in them if they conform to its requirement.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical argument about the identity of individual substances. The author begins by stating that the animal which is a human individual (e.g., Zayd) is constituted from matter and form specific to him. He then considers two possibilities: either the individual is fully realized by being from this particular matter and form, resulting in that specific individual, or not, in which case neither that individual man nor that individual animal comes to be. The author rejects the alternative notion that a human could become a man by accidental properties attached to matter, because the very same thing that made him a man also made him an animal—the two are inseparable in the individual. The argument is then extended to all species: the horse, for example, cannot change its horseness into humanity, as the matter and form are tied to the species. The author emphasizes that the same animal cannot be both a horse and a human simultaneously. The passage concludes with a methodological note: it is not the logician's job to provide examples for every species of every genus, but rather to give a general law that guides the application of the rule to specific cases. This guards against the misconception that species change is possible, a key principle in metaphysics and logic regarding the stability of natures.

صورة

الفرسية

Modes of Conception of the Five Universals
Author begins classification of the five universals by how their meanings are conceived.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify each of the five universals according to its mode of conception (bi-sharṭ, lā bi-sharṭ, or as a distinct type) based on the criteria in the passage.

Core Idea
The five universals are distinguished by how their meanings are conceived—with condition, without condition, or obtaining by itself.

The author begins by explaining that not all universals are conceived in the same way. Some are considered in isolation from anything else, some allow for accompaniment, and some are indivisible in terms of meaning. Understanding these modes is crucial for correctly identifying whether a term is a genus, species, differentia, property, or accident in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three modes described
Read the Arabic passage and find the three distinct ways a universal's meaning can be conceived: (1) 'with condition' (bi-sharṭ) that the meaning alone is considered, (2) 'without condition' (lā bi-sharṭ) that the meaning can be accompanied, and (3) a type that obtains by itself or by addition and is said only of things differing in number.
The third type is not explicitly named but is contrasted with the first two.
2
Match each mode to the corresponding universal
According to the classical logical tradition, the first mode (bi-sharṭ) corresponds to differentia (faṣl), because differentia is conceived as the specific distinguishing feature isolated from the genus. The second mode (lā bi-sharṭ) corresponds to genus (jins) and species (nawʿ), as they are predicated of individuals together with their differentiae or accidents. The third mode corresponds to property (khāṣṣa) and general accident (ʿaraḍ ʿāmm), which apply to individuals of a single species and do not vary across different natures.
This mapping is based on the criteria of predictability and accompaniment described in the passage.
3
Verify with an example from Islamic sciences
Take the term 'living being' (ḥayy) in the definition of 'human'. As a genus, it is conceived without condition: it applies to humans, animals, etc., even when accompanied by differentiae. The differentia 'rational' (nāṭiq) is conceived with condition: it is what distinguishes humans, and when isolated, it is not predicated of the aggregate 'human' without its genus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني : في الكليات الخمس واعلم أولاً بأن منها ما قد يتصور معناه بشرط أن يكون ذلك المعنى وحده، ويكون ما يقارنه زائدا عليه، ولا يكون ذلك المعنى مقولا على ذلك المجموع، وقد يتصور معناه لا بشرط أن يكون ذلك المعنى وحده بل مع احتمال أن يقارنه غيره وأن لا يفارقه، ويكون ذلك المعنى مقولا على ذلك المجموع حالة المقارنة. وهذا الآخر فلا يتحصل بنفسه؛ بل يكون محتملا لأن يقال على أشياء مختلفة الحقائق. وإنما يتحصل باتصاف ما يتخصص به حتى هو يصير بعينه واحدًا من تلك الأشياء، وقد يتحصل بنفسه أو بما يضاف إليه ولا يكون بهما ولا محتملا لأن يقال على أشياء مختلفة الحقائق؛ بل يقال على أشياء مختلفة بالعدد فقط. وهو أن يكون مشتركًا في أن المعنى الأول
The second type: on the five universals. Know firstly that among them there is that whose meaning is conceived on condition that that meaning is alone, and what accompanies it is additional to it, and that meaning is not predicated of that aggregate. And there is that whose meaning is conceived not on condition that that meaning is alone, but with the possibility that something else accompanies it and does not separate from it, and that meaning is predicated of that aggregate in the state of accompaniment. This latter does not obtain by itself; rather it is possible to be said of things of different natures. But it only obtains by the attribution of what particularizes it, until it becomes precisely one of those things. And there is that which obtains by itself or by what is added to it, and it is not such that by these two it is possible to be said of things of different natures; rather it is said of things differing only in number. And that is that it shares in the first meaning...
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage introduces the five universals (al-kulliyāt al-khams) by describing three modes of how their meanings are conceived. The author begins by stating that some universals are conceived 'with a condition' (bi-sharṭ) that the meaning alone is considered, and anything accompanying it is additional, so that meaning is not predicated of the aggregate. Another type is conceived 'without condition' (lā bi-sharṭ), where the meaning can be accompanied by something else that does not separate from it, and that meaning is predicated of the aggregate when accompanied. This latter type does not stand on its own but can be predicated of things of different natures; it only becomes concrete when combined with a specific attribute. Then there is a type that obtains by itself or by something added, and it is not subject to being said of things of different natures by these two modes; rather it is said only of things differing in number. The passage cuts off mid-sentence but seems to be distinguishing among the five universals: genus (jins), species (nawʿ), differentia (faṣl), property (khāṣṣa), and general accident (ʿaraḍ ʿāmm). The first mode with condition likely refers to differentia, the second without condition to genus or species, and the third to property or accident. This classification is foundational for defining terms in logic.

The Three Logical Considerations of a Universal: Matter, Genus, Species
Author classifies universals into matter, genus, species, and addresses an objection about the number of divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a universal as matter, genus, or species based on its logical condition, and explain the objection that the classification yields six divisions.

Core Idea
A single universal shifts its logical role depending on how we conceive it — alone, with potential accompaniment, or fully determined.

In this passage, the author introduces a powerful framework for understanding how the same universal term can play different roles in definition and predication. You will learn to distinguish three logical states: the universal as raw material (madda), as a broader category (jins), and as a fully specified kind (naw'). The classification hinges on the conditions under which the universal is conceived — alone (bi-shart an la), without condition (la bi-shart), or as determinate by itself. We will also see the beginning of an objection that challenges the completeness of this division.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions for conceiving a universal
The author describes three ways: (1) with the condition that the meaning is alone and what accompanies it is additional; (2) not with that condition, i.e., with potential accompaniment; (3) determinate by itself or by what is added. Each corresponds to a different logical consideration.
2
Link each condition to the terms matter, genus, species
The universal under condition (1) is called matter (madda), under (2) genus (jins), under (3) species (naw'). Note: 'matter' here is logical substratum, not physical.
3
Apply the conditions to the example 'animal'
When 'animal' is taken alone (bi-shart an la), it is matter — a part of man. When taken with condition of being with 'rational', it is genus — predicated of the composite. When taken as specialized by 'rational' so that it is man himself, it is species.
The same term 'animal' plays three roles depending on the condition.
4
Distinguish differentia (fasl) and accident ('arad) in accompaniments
When the accompanying element gives subsistence to the meaning, it is called differentia (fasl); when it attaches after subsistence, it is an accident ('arad). This distinction is crucial for understanding essence vs. property.
5
Identify the objection and the incomplete response
A hypothetical objector says: if matter is a separate consideration, universals would be six divisions, not five. The author begins to respond by asserting that matter is always one of the five, but the text cuts off before completing the argument.
The lesson ends mid-sentence; the full response is on the next page.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني : في الكليات الخمس
واعلم أولاً بأن منها ما قد يتصور معناه بشرط أن يكون ذلك المعنى وحده، ويكون ما يقارنه زائدا عليه، ولا يكون ذلك المعنى مقولا على ذلك المجموع، وقد يتصور معناه لا بشرط أن يكون ذلك المعنى وحده بل مع احتمال أن يقارنه غيره وأن لا يفارقه، ويكون ذلك المعنى مقولا على ذلك المجموع حالة المقارنة. وهذا الآخر فلا يتحصل بنفسه؛ بل يكون محتملا لأن يقال على أشياء مختلفة الحقائق. وإنما يتحصل باتصاف ما يتخصص به حتى هو يصير بعينه واحدًا من تلك الأشياء، وقد يتحصل بنفسه أو بما يضاف إليه ولا يكون بهما ولا محتملا لأن يقال على أشياء مختلفة الحقائق؛ بل يقال على أشياء مختلفة بالعدد فقط. وهو أن يكون مشتركًا في أن المعنى الأول يقال على الحاصل بعد لحوق الغير به، غير أن اللاحق به يعطي القوام لذلك المعنى في الصورة الأولى ويسمى فصلا، واللاحق به بعد التقوم في الصورة الآخرة يسمى عارضًا. فالكلي بالاعتبار الأول يسمى مادة، وبالاعتبار الثاني جنسا، وبالاعتبار الثالث نوعًا. فالحيوان مثلا، إذا أخذ بشرط أن لا يكون مع شيء وإن اقترن به الناطق مثلا صار المجموع مركبًا من الحيوان والناطق ولا يقال له إنه حيوان كان مادة. وإذا أخذ بشرط أن يكون مع شيء كالناطق مثلا، فإنه إذا تحصص به يصير ويقال له إنه حيوان كان جنسًا. وإذا أخذ أن يكون مع الناطق متخصصًا ومتحملًا كان نوعًا. فالحيوان في الأول جزء الإنسان ويتقدمه تقدم الجزيء في الوجود الذهني والخارجي، وفي الثاني ليس بجزئه لأن الجزء لا يُحمل على الكل لما نبينه من بعد؛ بل هو جزء من حده، ولا يؤخذ من حيث هو كذلك إلا في الذهن ويتقدمه فيه بالطبع لكنه في الخارج يتأخر عنه؛ لأن الإنسان ما لم يوجد لم يعقل له شيء يعمه وغيره وشيء يخصه ويصيره هو هو بعينه. وفي الثالث هو الإنسان نفسه؛ لأنه مأخوذ مع الناطق، والأشياء التي تضاف إليه بعد تقومه لا تفيده اختلافًا في الماهية؛ بل ربما تجعله مختلفا بالعدد نحو الإنسان الأسود وكهذا الإنسان، وذلك كذلك. فظهر الفرق بين الأشياء التي تدخل معنى وتجعله أشياء مختلفة الحقائق وبين الأشياء التي تدخل عليه وتجعله أشياء متفقة الحقائق.
ثم لقائل أن يقول : لو كان كما ذكرتم لكان الكلي على ستة أقسام؛ وذلك لأنه من حيث إن يكون مادة لا يكون جنسًا ولا نوعًا ولا فصلا ولا عرضًا عاما ولا خاصة كذلك، وهذا على خلاف ما اتفقوا عليه. لكنا نقول: ما يكون مادة للشيء وهو كلي فذلك واحد من هذه الخمسة لا محالة جنسًا كان أو غير جنس، ولو كان كذلك لكان من الممتنع أن يكون ذلك الكلي زائدا على
Second type: On the Five Universals

Know first that among them (the universals) there is what its meaning is conceived with the condition that that meaning is alone, and what accompanies it is additional to it, and that meaning is not predicated of that composite. And there is what its meaning is conceived not with the condition that that meaning is alone, but with the possibility that something else accompanies it and does not separate from it, and that meaning is predicated of that composite at the time of accompaniment. This latter does not become determinate by itself; rather it is possible to be predicated of things of different realities. It becomes determinate only by being qualified by what specializes it until it itself becomes one of those things. And there is what becomes determinate by itself or by what is added to it, and it is not such by these two, nor possible to be predicated of things of different realities; rather it is predicated of things differing only in number. And that is that it is common in that the first meaning is predicated of what results after the attachment of another, except that the attached gives subsistence to that meaning in the first form and is called a differentia (fasl), and the attached after subsistence in the second form is called an accident ('arad). So the universal in the first consideration is called matter (madda), in the second consideration genus (jins), and in the third consideration species (naw'). For example, 'animal': when taken with the condition that it is not with something, even if 'rational' accompanies it, the composite becomes composed of animal and rational, and it is not said of it that it is animal, so it was matter. And when taken with the condition that it is with something like 'rational', then when it specializes by it, it becomes and is said of it that it is animal, so it was genus. And when taken that it is with rational as specialized and carrying, it was species. So animal in the first is a part of man and precedes it as the part precedes in mental and external existence; in the second it is not a part of it, because the part is not predicated of the whole as we will explain later; rather it is a part of its definition, and it is not taken insofar as it is such except in the mind, and it precedes it naturally in the mind, but in the external it is posterior to it, because as long as man does not exist, nothing is conceived that includes him and others and something that specifies him and makes him exactly what he is. And in the third it is man himself, because it is taken with rational, and the things added to it after its subsistence do not give it difference in essence, but perhaps make it different by number, like the black man and such man, and so on. So the difference is apparent between things that enter a meaning and make it things of different realities and things that enter upon it and make it things of agreeing realities.

Then an objector may say: If it were as you mentioned, then the universal would have six divisions; because insofar as it is matter, it is not genus, nor species, nor differentia, nor common accident, nor special accident, and this contradicts what they agreed upon. But we say: What is matter for a thing and is universal, that is necessarily one of these five, whether it be genus or not, and if it were so, it would be impossible for that universal to be additional to...
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage defines the classification of universals into three logical considerations: matter (madda), genus (jins), and species (naw'). The author begins by describing three conditions under which a universal can be conceived: (1) with the condition that it is alone (bi-shart an la), (2) without condition of being alone (la bi-shart), and (3) as determinate by itself. These correspond to the universal as matter, genus, and species respectively. Using the example of 'animal', it is matter when taken alone (a part of man), genus when taken with the condition of being with a differentia like 'rational' (predicated of the composite), and species when taken as specialized by the differentia (identical to man). The passage then clarifies that the differentia (fasl) gives subsistence in the first case, while accidents give only numerical difference. Finally, an objection is raised: if matter is a category, then universals would be six, not five. The author begins to respond by stating that matter is always one of the five, but the response is cut off at the end of the page.

The Five Universals

Three Considerations of the Universal: Matter, Genus, Species
Author distinguishes three considerations of the universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the three considerations of a universal—as matter, genus, and species—and apply them to examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
One universal, three lenses: matter (unconditioned), genus (conditioned), species (combined).

This passage delves into the heart of how logicians in the Islamic tradition analyze universals. The same universal can be viewed in three distinct ways: as the raw material (madda) before any combination, as the genus (jins) when considered with a specifying attribute, and as the species (naw') when fully combined. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for precise definition in every Islamic science, from usul al-fiqh to kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three considerations
Locate in the Arabic where the author names 'matter', 'genus', and 'species' as the three considerations of the universal. Note the phrases: 'فالكلي بالاعتبار الأول يسمى مادة، وبالاعتبار الثاني جنسا، وبالاعتبار الثالث نوعًا'
2
Extract the defining condition for each consideration
For matter: the universal is taken without any condition (بشرط أن لا يكون مع شيء). For genus: it is taken with the condition that it is with a specifying attribute (بشرط أن يكون مع شيء). For species: it is taken as already specified and carried along (مأخوذ مع الناطق متخصصًا ومتحملًا).
3
Apply the conditions to the author's example
Take 'animal' and 'rational'. When animal is considered without any attachment, it is matter. When considered as being with rational (so that 'animal' is predicated of man), it is genus. When considered as already combined with rational (so that it is the species 'man'), it is species.
4
Analyze the relationships (part, predicate, whole)
The author explains: in the first consideration (matter), the universal is a part of the species and precedes it. In the second (genus), it is not a part but is predicated of the species. In the third (species), it is the species itself because it is taken with its differentia.
5
Test with a new example from Islamic sciences
Take the universal 'ibadah. Consider it: as matter (without any condition), as genus (with the condition of being an act of worship), and as species (e.g., salat as a specific type of ibadah). Verify which consideration applies in different contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقال على الحاصل بعد لحوق الغير به، غير أن اللاحق به يعطي القوام لذلك المعنى في الصورة الأولى ويسمى فصلا، واللاحق به بعد التقوم في الصورة الآخرة يسمى عارضًا. فالكلي بالاعتبار الأول يسمى مادة، وبالاعتبار الثاني جنسا، وبالاعتبار الثالث نوعًا. فالحيوان مثلا، إذا أخذ بشرط أن لا يكون مع شيء وإن اقترن به الناطق مثلا صار المجموع مركبًا من الحيوان والناطق ولا يقال له إنه حيوان كان مادة. وإذا أخذ بشرط أن يكون مع شيء كالناطق مثلا، فإنه إذا تحصص به يصير ويقال له إنه حيوان كان جنسًا. وإذا أخذ أن يكون مع الناطق متخصصًا ومتحملًا كان نوعًا. فالحيوان في الأول جزء الإنسان ويتقدمه تقدم الجزيء في الوجود الذهني والخارجي، وفي الثاني ليس بجزئه لأن الجزء لا يُحمل على الكل لما نبينه من بعد؛ بل هو جزء من حده، ولا يؤخذ من حيث هو كذلك إلا في الذهن ويتقدمه فيه بالطبع لكنه في الخارج يتأخر عنه؛ لأن الإنسان ما لم يوجد لم يعقل له شيء يعمه وغيره وشيء يخصه ويصيره هو هو بعينه. وفي الثالث هو الإنسان نفسه؛ لأنه مأخوذ مع الناطق، والأشياء التي تضاف إليه بعد تقومه لا تفيده اختلافًا في الماهية؛ بل ربما تجعله مختلفا بالعدد نحو الإنسان الأسود وكهذا الإنسان، وذلك كذلك. فظهر الفرق بين الأشياء التي تدخل معنى وتجعله أشياء مختلفة الحقائق وبين الأشياء التي تدخل عليه وتجعله أشياء متفقة الحقائق.
It is said of what is obtained after the attachment of another to it, but the attached gives subsistence to that meaning in the first form and is called a differentia (fasl), and the attached after subsistence in the second form is called an accident ('arad). So the universal in the first consideration is called matter, in the second consideration genus, and in the third consideration species. For example, animal, if taken with the condition that it is not with anything, even if rational, for example, is attached to it, then the composite becomes composed of animal and rational and it is not said of it that it is animal — it is matter. And if taken with the condition that it is with something like rational, then when it becomes specified by it, it becomes and is said of it that it is animal — it is genus. And if taken as being with rational, specified and carried, it is species. So animal in the first is a part of man and precedes him as a part precedes in mental and external existence, and in the second it is not a part of him because the part is not predicated of the whole as we will explain later; rather it is a part of his definition, and it is not taken as such except in the mind and precedes him there by nature, but in the external it is later than him; because as long as man does not exist, nothing that is common to him and others and something that is particular to him and makes him exactly himself is conceived. And in the third it is man himself because it is taken with rational, and things that are added to him after his subsistence do not give him difference in essence; rather perhaps they make him different by number like black man and this man, and that such. So the difference appeared between things that enter into a meaning and make it things differing in realities and between things that enter upon it and make it things agreeing in realities.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage defines the three ways a universal can be considered: as matter (madda), genus (jins), and species (naw'). The author begins by describing that when an attribute attaches to a thing and gives it subsistence, it is called a differentia (fasl); if it attaches after subsistence, it is called an accident. Then he applies this to the universal: considered purely without any condition, it is matter; considered with the condition that it is with a specifying attribute, it is genus; considered as already specified and combined with the attribute, it is species. He illustrates with the example of 'animal' and 'rational' in the composition of man. In the first consideration, animal is a part of man and precedes him; in the second, it is predicated of man (since man is animal) but is not a part; in the third, animal is essentially man himself when taken together with rational. The passage concludes by distinguishing between things that change essence and things that only change accidents.

Key Terms
مادة
جنس
نوع

A) genus
B) matter
C) species
D) differentia

Key Takeaway
The universal is one but can be considered in three distinct ways: as matter (unconditioned), as genus (conditioned to be with an attribute), and as species (already combined).

Pick a term from fiqh (e.g., 'ibadah) and identify which consideration applies in a specific legal context (e.g., the genus of 'ibadah vs. the species of salat).

Refuting the Objection of Six Universals and Enumerating the Five
Author refutes the objection of six universals and establishes the five.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the objection that the universal is sixfold and classify any universal into one of the five universals (genus, species, differentia, general accident, particular accident).

Core Idea
The universal is not six but five; matter is always one of the five.

The author anticipates an objection: if the universal is considered as 'matter', it would be a sixth category, making the universal sixfold. He refutes this by showing that whatever is matter for a thing is necessarily one of the five universals. Then he enumerates the five universals with their definitions, establishing the standard classification used in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claim
Find the objector's statement: he says that if the universal were as described, it would be six divisions because matter is not among the five. This would contradict the agreed-upon five.
The objector assumes matter is a separate category.
2
Understand the refutation
The author replies that matter for a thing is always one of the five universals (genus, species, differentia, general accident, or particular accident). It is impossible for a universal to be additional to these five.
This shows the objector's confusion: matter is not a sixth universal but is always one of the known five.
3
Analyze the fivefold classification
The author divides universals into three categories: (1) complete essence (species), (2) parts within the essence (genus and differentia), and (3) parts outside the essence (general accident and particular accident). Each is defined by what it answers in 'What is it?' and its scope.
This classification is exhaustive: every universal falls into one of these five.
4
Test with examples
Apply the classification: 'man' is a species (complete essence), 'animal' is a genus (general part of essence), 'rational' is a differentia (particular part of essence), 'mobile' is a general accident (outside, common), and 'laughter' is a particular accident (outside, specific).
This confirms that no universal is left out.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لقائل أن يقول : لو كان كما ذكرتم لكان الكلي على ستة أقسام؛ وذلك لأنه من حيث إن يكون مادة لا يكون جنسًا ولا نوعًا ولا فصلا ولا عرضًا عاما ولا خاصة كذلك، وهذا على خلاف ما اتفقوا عليه. لكنا نقول: ما يكون مادة للشيء وهو كلي فذلك واحد من هذه الخمسة لا محالة جنسًا كان أو غير جنس، ولو كان كذلك لكان من الممتنع أن يكون ذلك الكلي زائدا على هذه الخمسة؛ بل الكلي منحصر فيها كما هو المسطور في الكتب أنه لا يخلو إما أن يكون تمام الماهية كالنوع وهو الذي يقال على متفقي الطباع في جواب ما هو ؟ كالإنسان، أو داخلا فيها. وإنه إما أن يكون عاما يقال على مختلفي الطباع في جواب «ما هو ؟»، وهو «الجنس» إذا كان المشترك تمامه هو نحو الحيوان وإلا فهو جزؤه؛ أو خاصا يقال على ما يقال عليه النوع فقط، وهو الفصل» إذا كان المميز تمامه هو كالناطق وإلا فهو جزؤه، أو خارجًا عنها. وإنه إما أن يكون عاما يقال على ما يقال عليه الجنس وهو العرض العام نحو المتحرك، أو خاصًا يقال على ما يقال عليه الفصل وهو الخاصة كالضاحك. وما يكون من الأقسام المذكورة ، أي قسم كان فذلك في نفسه تمام ماهية، إما الشخص واحد يدل عليه اللفظ بحسب الخصوصية المحضة كما في المعين من الأشخاص، أو الأشخاص مختلفة في الماهية واللفظ الدال عليه هو الدال بحسب الشركة المحضة في المعين من الأشخاص، أو الأشخاص متفقة في الماهية واللفظ الدال عليه هو الدال بحسب الشركة والخصوصية معا كما في المعين من الأنواع.
Then a speaker may say: If it were as you mentioned, the universal would be six divisions; because inasmuch as it is matter, it is not genus, nor species, nor differentia, nor general accident, nor particular accident, and this is contrary to what they agreed upon. But we say: What is matter for a thing while it is a universal, that is necessarily one of these five, whether it is genus or other than genus; and if it were so, it would be impossible for that universal to be additional to these five; rather the universal is confined to them as is written in the books: that it is not free from either being the complete essence like the species, which is said of things agreeing in nature in answer to 'What is it?' like man; or being within it. And it is either general, said of things differing in nature in answer to 'What is it?', and it is 'genus' if the common thing is its entirety like animal, otherwise it is part of it; or particular, said only of that of which the species is said, and it is 'differentia' if the distinguisher is its entirety like rational, otherwise it is part of it; or outside it. And it is either general, said of that of which the genus is said, and it is the general accident like mobile; or particular, said of that of which the differentia is said, and it is the particular accident like laughter. And whatever is of the mentioned divisions, any division it may be, that is in itself a complete essence: either a single person indicated by the word according to pure particularity as in a determinate individual; or individuals differing in essence and the word indicating it indicates according to pure commonality in the determinate individuals; or individuals agreeing in essence and the word indicating it indicates according to commonality and particularity together as in a determinate species.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its refutation. The objector claims that if the universal is considered as matter (which is not one of the five universals), then there would be six divisions: the five plus matter. The author replies that matter is always one of the five — whatever is matter for a thing is necessarily a genus or differentia or species or accident. Then he proceeds to enumerate the five universals: species (naw') which is the complete essence, genus (jins) which is the general part of the essence, differentia (fasl) which is the particular part of the essence, general accident ('arad 'amm) which is outside the essence and common, and particular accident (khasa) which is outside and particular. He further explains that each of these is a complete essence in itself, and describes how the word may indicate individual or shared meanings.

Genus and Differentia in Definitions
Author clarifies when genus and differentia are used in definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a term functions as genus (jins) vs. differentia (fasl) in composite definitions based on the question it answers ('What is it?' vs. 'Which is it?').

Core Idea
Genus and differentia switch roles depending on the essence.

This lesson examines a subtle rule: genus as such answers 'What is it?', not 'Which is it?', and differentia as such answers 'Which is it?', not 'What is it?'. However, in composite essences, a single term can play both roles relative to different things. The author illustrates this with the example of 'animal' in the definition of man.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two questions
The question 'What is it?' (ma huwa) asks for the essence or genus. The question 'Which is it?' (ayy ma huwa) asks for the distinguishing differentia. Recognize that these are distinct logical inquiries.
2
Apply the basic rule
Genus, as such, is not said in answer to 'Which is it?' and differentia, as such, is not said in answer to 'What is it?'. This means each has its proper role: genus gives the kind, differentia gives the distinctive property.
The passage explicitly states this rule.
3
Examine a composite essence
When an essence has two components, one may be more general than the thing defined and more specific than something else. In such cases, that term can answer both questions depending on the context. For example, 'animal' is more general than 'man' but more specific than 'angel' if angel is also rational.
The author uses the composite essence of man.
4
Analyse the given example
For man, 'animal' functions as genus (answering 'What is it?') relative to man, but relative to angel (if angel is rational), 'animal' would function as differentia (answering 'Which is it?') because it distinguishes angel from other rational beings. The author notes that the belief that angel is rational is false, so this is only a hypothetical illustration.
Focus on the logical point, not the theological accuracy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الجنس من حيث هو هو لا يكون مقولا في جواب «أي ما هو ؟»، والفصل من حيث هو هو لا يكون مقولا في جواب ما هو ؟ » كذلك. فأما إذا كانت الماهية مركبة من الشيئين اللذين أحدهما أعم منه بالنسبة إلى شيء وأخص منه بالنسبة إلى شيء آخر، فيكون كل واحد منهما مقولاً في جواب «ما هو ؟» تارة وفي جواب «أي ما هو ؟ [تارة] أخرى. كما قيل في ماهية الإنسان: «إنها مركبة من حيوان وناطق ، والحيوان أعم منه بالنسبة إلى الفرس مثلا، وأخص منه بالنسبة إلى الملك عند من يعتقد أن الإنسان مع الملك يشتركان في كون كل واحد منهما ناطقا، وإن كان ذلك الاعتقاد اعتقادًا باطلا عند الحكماء
Then genus, inasmuch as it is genus, is not said in answer to 'Which is it?', and differentia, inasmuch as it is differentia, is not said in answer to 'What is it?' likewise. But if the essence is composed of two things, one of which is more general than it with respect to something and more particular than it with respect to something else, then each of them is said in answer to 'What is it?' sometimes and in answer to 'Which is it?' sometimes. As was said about the essence of man: 'It is composed of animal and rational', and animal is more general than it with respect to horse, for example, and more particular than it with respect to angel, according to those who believe that man and angel share in each being rational, even though that belief is false according to the philosophers.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage clarifies the roles of genus and differentia in definition. Genus (jins) as such is not said in answer to 'Which is it?' (ayy ma huwa) because it is a general category; differentia (fasl) as such is not said in answer to 'What is it?' (ma huwa) because it does not give the essence. However, when an essence is composite, a term like 'animal' can function as genus relative to man (since it is more general) and as differentia relative to angel (if angel is also rational). The author notes that this latter view (that man and angel share rationality) is false in philosophy, but it serves as an illustration.

جواب ما هو

Key Takeaway
A term's role as genus or differentia is relative to the essence being defined and the broader context of comparison.

The Five Universals

Classification of the Five Universals Relative to the Essence
Author classifies the five universals as whole essence, part of essence, or outside essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify each of the five universals as whole essence, part of essence, or outside essence and provide an Islamic example for each.

Core Idea
The five universals are defined by their relation to the essence: species is the whole, genus and differentia are parts, accidents are outside.

After introducing the five universals, this passage systematically divides them based on their relationship to the essence. Understanding this division is essential for crafting precise definitions in fiqh, kalam, and usul al-fiqh. Let's walk through the classification step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three main categories
The passage states that every universal either constitutes the whole essence, is a part of the essence, or lies outside the essence. Start by recognizing these three groups.
The whole essence is the species (al-naw‘), parts are genus (al-jins) and differentia (al-fasl), and outside are common accident (al-‘arad al-‘āmm) and proper accident (al-khāṣṣa).
2
Analyze the part-of-essence category
Within the part-of-essence category, the universal may be general (said of different natures) or specific (said only of what the species is said of). General gives genus, specific gives differentia. Note the condition: if the shared element is the whole essence, it is genus; if only part, it is a part of genus.
3
Analyze the outside-essence category
Outside essence divides into general (common accident, said of what genus is said of) and specific (proper accident, said of what differentia is said of). Examples from the text: 'mobile' for common accident, 'capable of laughter' for proper accident.
4
Test with an Islamic example
Take the species 'human' in Islamic theology. Its essence is compound. 'Animal' is genus (part of essence, general), 'rational' is differentia (part of essence, specific). 'Having knowledge' would be a common accident (outside, general), while 'being the addressee of divine commands' is a proper accident (outside, specific). Verify each classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذه الخمسة؛ بل الكلي منحصر فيها كما هو المسطور في الكتب أنه لا يخلو إما أن يكون تمام الماهية كالنوع وهو الذي يقال على متفقي الطباع في جواب ما هو ؟ كالإنسان، أو داخلا فيها. وإنه إما أن يكون عاما يقال على مختلفي الطباع في جواب «ما هو»، وهو «الجنس» إذا كان المشترك تمامه هو نحو الحيوان وإلا فهو جزؤه؛ أو خاصا يقال على ما يقال عليه النوع فقط، وهو الفصل» إذا كان المميز تمامه هو كالناطق وإلا فهو جزؤه، أو خارجًا عنها. وإنه إما أن يكون عاما يقال على ما يقال عليه الجنس وهو العرض العام نحو المتحرك، أو خاصًا يقال على ما يقال عليه الفصل وهو الخاصة كالضاحك. وما يكون من الأقسام المذكورة، أي قسم كان فذلك في نفسه تمام ماهية، إما الشخص واحد يدل عليه اللفظ بحسب الخصوصية المحضة كما في المعين من الأشخاص، أو الأشخاص مختلفة في الماهية واللفظ الدال عليه هو الدال بحسب الشركة المحضة في المعين من الأشخاص، أو الأشخاص متفقة في الماهية واللفظ الدال عليه هو الدال بحسب الشركة والخصوصية معا كما في المعين من الأنواع. ثم الجنس من حيث هو هو لا يكون مقولا في جواب «أي ما هو ؟»، والفصل من حيث هو هو لا يكون مقولا في جواب ما هو ؟ » كذلك. فأما إذا كانت الماهية مركبة من الشيئين اللذين أحدهما أعم منه بالنسبة إلى شيء وأخص منه بالنسبة إلى شيء آخر، فيكون كل واحد منهما مقولاً في جواب ما هو ؟» تارة وفي جواب «أي ما هو ؟ [تارة] أخرى. كما قيل في ماهية الإنسان: «إنها مركبة من حيوان وناطق ، والحيوان أعم منه بالنسبة إلى الفرس مثلا، وأخص منه بالنسبة إلى الملك عند من يعتقد أن الإنسان مع الملك يشتركان في كون كل واحد منهما ناطقا، وإن كان ذلك الاعتقاد اعتقادًا باطلا عند الحكماء وغيرهم من أهل التحقيق. فإن النطق للإنسان لا يكون كما يكون للملك وإن كان الملك ناطقا ، لكنه إذا كان كذلك كان للحيوان جنسا بالنسبة إلى الفرس، وفصلا بالنسبة إلى الملك. فيكون مقولا في جواب ما هو ؟ من حيث إنه جنس، ومقولا في جواب «أي ما هو؟» من حيث إنه فصل، وكذلك الناطق في هذه الصورة.
These five; rather, the universal is confined to them, as is written in the books: it is either the whole of the essence, like species, which is said of things agreeing in nature in answer to 'what is it?', like human; or it is within it. And it is either general, said of things differing in nature in answer to 'what is it?', and it is 'genus' if the common thing is whole like animal, otherwise it is part of it; or specific, said only of what species is said of, and it is 'differentia' if the distinguishing thing is whole like rational, otherwise it is part of it; or outside it. And it is either general, said of what genus is said of, and it is common accident like mobile; or specific, said of what differentia is said of, and it is proper accident like capable of laughter. And whatever is of the mentioned divisions, whichever division it is, it is in itself whole of essence, either a single person indicated by the word according to pure particularity, as in determinate individuals; or persons differing in essence and the word indicating them is according to pure commonality in determinate individuals; or persons agreeing in essence and the word indicating them is according to commonality and particularity together, as in determinate species. Then genus as such is not said in answer to 'which thing is it?', and differentia as such is not said in answer to 'what is it?' likewise. But if the essence is composed of two things, one of which is more general than it relative to something and more specific relative to something else, then each of them is said in answer to 'what is it?' sometimes and in answer to 'which thing is it?' other times. As was said about the essence of human: 'It is composed of animal and rational.' Animal is more general than it relative to, for example, horse, and more specific than it relative to angel according to those who believe that human and angel share in each of them being rational, even if that belief is a false belief according to the philosophers and others among the people of verification. For the rationality of human is not like that of angel, even if the angel is rational. But if it is so, then animal is genus relative to horse and differentia relative to angel. So it is said in answer to 'what is it?' insofar as it is genus, and said in answer to 'which thing is it?' insofar as it is differentia, and likewise rational in this situation.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage defines the division of the five universals relative to the essence. The author begins by stating that the universal is confined to these five, and then divides them. First, the universal may be the whole of the essence, which is the species (al-naw'), like 'human' said of individuals agreeing in nature in answer to 'what is it?'. Second, it may be part of the essence, and this part is either general (said of different natures) or specific (said only of what the species is said of). The general part is the genus (al-jins) if the shared element is the whole essence (e.g., 'animal'), or a part of it if the shared element is not the whole. The specific part is the differentia (al-fasl) if the distinguishing element is the whole essence (e.g., 'rational'), or a part of it. Third, the universal may be outside the essence, either general (common accident, like 'mobile') or specific (proper accident, like 'capable of laughter'). The passage then clarifies that each of these divisions is itself a whole essence in its own right, and depending on the level, words can indicate individuals with pure particularity, pure commonality, or both. The author then discusses the special case where a thing like 'animal' can function as genus relative to one species (horse) and as differentia relative to another (angel), according to a false belief. This shows that genus and differentia are not absolute but relative. The commentary uses the example of human composed of animal and rational to illustrate how the same term can answer 'what is it?' when it is genus and 'which thing is it?' when it is differentia.

Key Terms
النوع
الجنس
الفصل
العرض العام
الخاصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Human
B) Animal
C) Mobile
D) Capable of laughter

Key Takeaway
The five universals are defined by their relation to the essence: species is the whole, genus and differentia are parts, common and proper accidents are outside.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Relativity of Genus and Differentia in Composite Essences
Author discusses relativity of genus and differentia in composite essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify cases where a term functions as genus relative to one species and as differentia relative to another, using the example of the composite essence of humans.

Core Idea
Genus and differentia are relative: the same term can be genus for one species and differentia for another in a composite essence.

The passage introduces a special scenario where an essence is composed of two universals, such as human composed of animal and rational. In such cases, the same term—'animal'—can serve as genus when compared to horse, and as differentia when compared to angel. This insight refines our understanding of definitions and shows the relational nature of the universals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the composite essence
The passage uses the essence of human as composed of 'animal' and 'rational'. Recognize that this composite nature allows the same part to play different roles depending on the comparison.
2
Determine the comparative framework
Compare the term 'animal' relative to different species. Relative to horse, 'animal' is more general and answers 'what is it?', so it is genus. Relative to angel (under a certain false belief), 'animal' is more specific and answers 'which thing is it?', so it is differentia.
The passage notes that the belief that angels are rational is false, but uses the example to illustrate relativity.
3
Understand the two questions
In logic, 'what is it?' (ما هو) asks for the genus or species, while 'which thing is it?' (أي ما هو) asks for the differentia. The same term can answer different questions depending on context.
4
Apply to an Islamic concept
Consider the definition of 'prayer' (salah) in fiqh. 'Act of worship' is genus relative to 'prayer', but could be differentia relative to a broader category like 'devotional act' that includes dhikr. Find such relative shifts in usul al-fiqh texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذه الخمسة؛ بل الكلي منحصر فيها كما هو المسطور في الكتب أنه لا يخلو إما أن يكون تمام الماهية كالنوع وهو الذي يقال على متفقي الطباع في جواب ما هو ؟ كالإنسان، أو داخلا فيها. وإنه إما أن يكون عاما يقال على مختلفي الطباع في جواب «ما هو»، وهو «الجنس» إذا كان المشترك تمامه هو نحو الحيوان وإلا فهو جزؤه؛ أو خاصا يقال على ما يقال عليه النوع فقط، وهو الفصل» إذا كان المميز تمامه هو كالناطق وإلا فهو جزؤه، أو خارجًا عنها. وإنه إما أن يكون عاما يقال على ما يقال عليه الجنس وهو العرض العام نحو المتحرك، أو خاصًا يقال على ما يقال عليه الفصل وهو الخاصة كالضاحك. وما يكون من الأقسام المذكورة، أي قسم كان فذلك في نفسه تمام ماهية، إما الشخص واحد يدل عليه اللفظ بحسب الخصوصية المحضة كما في المعين من الأشخاص، أو الأشخاص مختلفة في الماهية واللفظ الدال عليه هو الدال بحسب الشركة المحضة في المعين من الأشخاص، أو الأشخاص متفقة في الماهية واللفظ الدال عليه هو الدال بحسب الشركة والخصوصية معا كما في المعين من الأنواع. ثم الجنس من حيث هو هو لا يكون مقولا في جواب «أي ما هو ؟»، والفصل من حيث هو هو لا يكون مقولا في جواب ما هو ؟ » كذلك. فأما إذا كانت الماهية مركبة من الشيئين اللذين أحدهما أعم منه بالنسبة إلى شيء وأخص منه بالنسبة إلى شيء آخر، فيكون كل واحد منهما مقولاً في جواب ما هو ؟» تارة وفي جواب «أي ما هو ؟ [تارة] أخرى. كما قيل في ماهية الإنسان: «إنها مركبة من حيوان وناطق ، والحيوان أعم منه بالنسبة إلى الفرس مثلا، وأخص منه بالنسبة إلى الملك عند من يعتقد أن الإنسان مع الملك يشتركان في كون كل واحد منهما ناطقا، وإن كان ذلك الاعتقاد اعتقادًا باطلا عند الحكماء وغيرهم من أهل التحقيق. فإن النطق للإنسان لا يكون كما يكون للملك وإن كان الملك ناطقا ، لكنه إذا كان كذلك كان للحيوان جنسا بالنسبة إلى الفرس، وفصلا بالنسبة إلى الملك. فيكون مقولا في جواب ما هو ؟ من حيث إنه جنس، ومقولا في جواب «أي ما هو؟» من حيث إنه فصل، وكذلك الناطق في هذه الصورة.
These five; rather, the universal is confined to them, as is written in the books: it is either the whole of the essence, like species, which is said of things agreeing in nature in answer to 'what is it?', like human; or it is within it. And it is either general, said of things differing in nature in answer to 'what is it?', and it is 'genus' if the common thing is whole like animal, otherwise it is part of it; or specific, said only of what species is said of, and it is 'differentia' if the distinguishing thing is whole like rational, otherwise it is part of it; or outside it. And it is either general, said of what genus is said of, and it is common accident like mobile; or specific, said of what differentia is said of, and it is proper accident like capable of laughter. And whatever is of the mentioned divisions, whichever division it is, it is in itself whole of essence, either a single person indicated by the word according to pure particularity, as in determinate individuals; or persons differing in essence and the word indicating them is according to pure commonality in determinate individuals; or persons agreeing in essence and the word indicating them is according to commonality and particularity together, as in determinate species. Then genus as such is not said in answer to 'which thing is it?', and differentia as such is not said in answer to 'what is it?' likewise. But if the essence is composed of two things, one of which is more general than it relative to something and more specific relative to something else, then each of them is said in answer to 'what is it?' sometimes and in answer to 'which thing is it?' other times. As was said about the essence of human: 'It is composed of animal and rational.' Animal is more general than it relative to, for example, horse, and more specific than it relative to angel according to those who believe that human and angel share in each of them being rational, even if that belief is a false belief according to the philosophers and others among the people of verification. For the rationality of human is not like that of angel, even if the angel is rational. But if it is so, then animal is genus relative to horse and differentia relative to angel. So it is said in answer to 'what is it?' insofar as it is genus, and said in answer to 'which thing is it?' insofar as it is differentia, and likewise rational in this situation.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage defines the division of the five universals relative to the essence. The author begins by stating that the universal is confined to these five, and then divides them. First, the universal may be the whole of the essence, which is the species (al-naw'), like 'human' said of individuals agreeing in nature in answer to 'what is it?'. Second, it may be part of the essence, and this part is either general (said of different natures) or specific (said only of what the species is said of). The general part is the genus (al-jins) if the shared element is the whole essence (e.g., 'animal'), or a part of it if the shared element is not the whole. The specific part is the differentia (al-fasl) if the distinguishing element is the whole essence (e.g., 'rational'), or a part of it. Third, the universal may be outside the essence, either general (common accident, like 'mobile') or specific (proper accident, like 'capable of laughter'). The passage then clarifies that each of these divisions is itself a whole essence in its own right, and depending on the level, words can indicate individuals with pure particularity, pure commonality, or both. The author then discusses the special case where a thing like 'animal' can function as genus relative to one species (horse) and as differentia relative to another (angel), according to a false belief. This shows that genus and differentia are not absolute but relative. The commentary uses the example of human composed of animal and rational to illustrate how the same term can answer 'what is it?' when it is genus and 'which thing is it?' when it is differentia.

الفصل

أي ما هو

Key Takeaway
Genus and differentia are relational: the same universal can serve as genus for one species and differentia for another when essences are composite.

Etymology and Definition of Genus (Jins)
Author traces the origin of the term 'jins' from Greek and gives the logical definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the semantic shift of the term 'jins' from its Greek roots to its logical definition and identify its definition as 'predicated of many different in species in answer to what is it?'.

Core Idea
The logical term 'jins' was borrowed from a social ancestor concept, redefined for universal predication.

When logicians speak of 'jins', they mean something very precise: a universal that answers 'what is it?' for things of different species. But where did this term come from? The author traces its journey from Greek kinship terms to a cutting-edge logical tool. Understanding this shift helps you appreciate the technical nature of the Five Universals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original Greek meaning
Find the examples of 'Alawite' and 'Egyptian' as ancestrally defined groups. The term 'jins' originally referred to a common ancestor or tribe.
These are social groupings based on descent.
2
Recognize the semantic transfer
Note how the term was first applied to the cause (e.g., Ali for Alawites, Egypt for Egyptians) and then to any universal participation.
The cause is considered more worthy of the name than the effect.
3
Extract the logical definition
Locate the definitive sentence at the end of the passage: 'the predicated of many different in species in answer to what is it?'
This is the technical definition used by logicians.
4
Apply the definition to an Islamic science example
Consider 'animal' as a jins for 'human' and 'horse'. Verify that it is said in answer to 'what is it?' and that the species differ.
This clarifies the scope of the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في الجنس] أما «الجنس» فاللفظة التي كانت تدل على معنى الجنس في اللغة اليونانية [1] كانت عندهم بحسب الوضع الأول على غير ذلك، [ب] ثم نقلت بالوضع الثاني إلى المعنى الذي يسمى عند أهل المنطق جنسًا. فكانوا يسمون المعنى الذي يشترك فيه الكثرة جنسًا نحو «العلوية» و«المصرية»؛ إذ العلوية كانت تسمى عندهم باسم الجنس، وبالقياس إلى أشخاس العلويين، وكذلك المصرية. وكانوا أيضًا / يسمون الواحد المنسوب الذي يشترك فيه الكثرة [٢٣ و] جنسا، وكان علي جنسًا للعلويين عندهم. وكان هذا القسم أولى عندهم بالجنسية لما أن عليا سبب لكون العلوية جنسًا، ومصرا للمصرية. ويظن أن السبب أولى بالاسم من المسبب، إذا وافقه في معناه وبسبب أنهم كانوا يسمون الحرف والصناعات أنفسها أجناسًا للمشتركين فيها، والشركة نفسها جنسًا أيضًا. فلما كان المعنى الذي يسمى الآن عند أهل المنطق جنسًا معقول واحد له نسبة إلى أشياء كثيرة تشترك فيه، ولم يكن له في الوضع الأول اسم نقل له من اسم هذه الأمور المتشابهة اسم فسمي جنسًا. وهو الذي يتكلم فيه المنطقيون ويرسمونه بأنه المقول على كثيرين مختلفين بالنوع في جواب «ما هو؟».
Chapter: [On Genus] As for 'genus', the word that indicated the meaning of genus in the Greek language [1] was, according to them, in the first usage on something else, then it was transferred by the second usage to the meaning that is called genus by the logicians. For they used to call the meaning in which the many participate 'genus', like 'Alawite' and 'Egyptian'; since 'Alawite' was called among them by the name of genus, and by analogy to the individuals of the Alawites, and likewise 'Egyptian'. And they also used to call the one relative being in which the many participate [23w] 'genus', and 'Ali' was a genus for the Alawites according to them. And this type was more worthy among them of being genus because Ali was the cause of 'Alawite' being a genus, and Egypt for 'Egyptian'. And it is thought that the cause is more worthy of the name than the effect, if it agrees with it in meaning, and because they used to call the crafts and occupations themselves 'genera' for those who share in them, and the participation itself 'genus' as well. So when the meaning that is now called genus by the logicians is a single intelligible having a relation to many things that participate in it, and it had no name in the first usage, a name was transferred to it from the name of these similar things, and it was called 'genus'. And this is what the logicians speak about and define it as 'the predicated of many different in species in answer to “what is it?”'.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is a chapter on genus (al-jins). The author begins by explaining the linguistic origin of the term 'jins' in Greek. Originally, it referred to a common ancestor or group, like 'Alawite' (descendants of Ali) or 'Egyptian' (people of Egypt). The word was then transferred to the logical meaning of a universal predicated of many different in species. The author notes that the cause (e.g., Ali for Alawites) is more worthy of the name 'genus' than the effect (the group). Eventually, the logicians adopted the term for the concept of a universal that applies to many things differing in species, and they define it as 'the predicated of many different in species in answer to “what is it?”'. This definition is illustrated with examples from the Islamic sciences: for instance, 'animal' is a genus for humans and horses, and it is said in answer to 'what is it?' when asked about either.

The logical definition of jins as 'predicated of many different in species in answer to what is it' is a technical transfer from a social ancestor term, refined for universal predication.

Natiq as Differentia and Genus
Author illustrates how 'natiq' functions as differentia for humans but genus for angels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a universal functions as a genus or differentia in a given logical context using the example of 'natiq'.

Core Idea
A single universal can be a differentia for one species and a genus for another.

This passage from the logic text demonstrates that the same term, 'natiq' (rational), can play different logical roles depending on what it is predicated of. Understanding this relativity is crucial for constructing accurate definitions in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal in question
The passage focuses on the term 'natiq' (rational). What is the term being analyzed?
2
Recognize the two contexts
Note that 'natiq' is predicated of humans and angels in different ways. For humans, it is a distinguishing feature; for angels, it is a shared category.
The passage says: 'النطق للإنسان لا يكون كما يكون للملك' - 'natiq for humans is not as it is for an angel.'
3
Apply the rule for genus vs. differentia
Recall that a differentia answers 'which thing is it?' within a genus, while a genus answers 'what is it?' and encompasses species. Determine which role 'natiq' plays in each context.
When predicated of humans, 'natiq' distinguishes them from other animals: it is a differentia. When predicated of angels (another rational species), 'natiq' could serve as a genus encompassing different types of angels.
4
Conclude the logical role
Based on the passage, conclude that 'natiq' is a differentia for humans and a potential genus for angels. This illustrates the relativity of the five universals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وغيرهم من أهل التحقيق. فإن النطق للإنسان لا يكون كما يكون للملك وإن كان الملك ناطقا ، لكنه إذا كان كذلك كان للحيوان جنسا بالنسبة إلى الفرس، وفصلا بالنسبة إلى الملك. فيكون مقولا في جواب ما هو ؟ من حيث إنه جنس، ومقولا في جواب «أي ما هو؟» من حيث إنه فصل، وكذلك الناطق في هذه الصورة.
And others from among the people of verification. For 'natiq' (rational) for humans is not as it is for an angel, even if the angel is rational. However, if it were so, it would be a genus for 'animal' relative to 'horse', and a differentia relative to 'angel'. So it is predicated in answer to 'what is it?' inasmuch as it is a genus, and predicated in answer to 'which thing is it?' inasmuch as it is a differentia. And likewise 'natiq' in this form.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage explains the flexibility of the terms 'genus' and 'differentia' (both of the five universals). The author uses the example of 'natiq' (rational) to show that a single attribute can function as a genus for one species and a differentia for another. For humans, 'natiq' is a differentia distinguishing them from other animals. But for angels (who are also rational), 'natiq' could be a genus under which different species of angels fall. The author states that if 'natiq' were considered a genus for 'animal' (a higher genus), then relative to 'horse' it would be a genus, but relative to 'angel' it would be a differentia. The point is that the logical role depends on the context of predication: when answering 'what is it?' it is a genus, and when answering 'which thing is it?' (asking for the differentia) it is a differentia. This illustrates the principle that universals are relative.

Key Terms
الناطق (al-nāṭiq): In this passage, 'natiq' (rational) is shown to be a differentia for humans and a potential genus for angels, depending on the logical relation. [Note: No significant shift; this is the standard usage in the five universals.]
الجنس (al-jins): A universal predicated of many differing in species in answer to 'what is it?' - here shown to be relative. [Note: The term is stable, but the author emphasizes its relativity.]
الفصل (al-faṣl): A universal predicated of many differing in species in answer to 'which thing is it?' - the differentia. [Note: Standard usage.]

The same universal can play different roles as genus or differentia depending on the context of predication.
